<meta name="DC.Title" content="Consequences of Whole-Genome Triplication as Revealed by Comparative Genomic Analyses of the Wild Radish Raphanus raphanistrum and Three Other Brassicaceae Species" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1105/tpc.114.124297" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2014-05-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2014 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Polyploidization events are frequent among flowering plants, and the duplicate genes produced via such events contribute significantly to plant evolution. We sequenced the genome of wild radish ( Raphanus raphanistrum ), a Brassicaceae species that experienced a whole-genome triplication event prior to diverging from Brassica rapa . Despite substantial gene gains in these two species compared with Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata , ∼70% of the orthologous groups experienced gene losses in R. raphanistrum and B. rapa , with most of the losses occurring prior to their divergence. The retained duplicates show substantial divergence in sequence and expression. Based on comparison of A. thaliana and R. raphanistrum ortholog floral expression levels, retained radish duplicates diverged primarily via maintenance of ancestral expression level in one copy and reduction of expression level in others. In addition, retained duplicates differed significantly from genes that reverted to singleton state in function, sequence composition, expression patterns, network connectivity, and rates of evolution. Using these properties, we established a statistical learning model for predicting whether a duplicate would be retained postpolyploidization. Overall, our study provides new insights into the processes of plant duplicate loss, retention, and functional divergence and highlights the need for further understanding factors controlling duplicate gene fate.

*   ### Glossary
    
    mya
    :   million years ago
    WGD
    :   whole-genome duplication
    WGT
    :   whole-genome triplication
    OG
    :   orthologous group
    SVM
    :   Support Vector Machine
    AUC
    :   Area Under Curve
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Polyploidization events are frequent among flowering plants, and the duplicate genes produced via such events contribute significantly to plant evolution. We sequenced the genome of wild radish (&lt;i&gt;Raphanus raphanistrum&lt;/i&gt;), a Brassicaceae species that experienced a whole-genome triplication event prior to diverging from &lt;i&gt;Brassica rapa&lt;/i&gt;. Despite substantial gene gains in these two species compared with &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis lyrata&lt;/i&gt;, ∼70% of the orthologous groups experienced gene losses in &lt;i&gt;R. raphanistrum&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;B. rapa&lt;/i&gt;, with most of the losses occurring prior to their divergence. The retained duplicates show substantial divergence in sequence and expression. Based on comparison of &lt;i&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;R. raphanistrum&lt;/i&gt; ortholog floral expression levels, retained radish duplicates diverged primarily via maintenance of ancestral expression level in one copy and reduction of expression level in others. In addition, retained duplicates differed significantly from genes that reverted to singleton state in function, sequence composition, expression patterns, network connectivity, and rates of evolution. Using these properties, we established a statistical learning model for predicting whether a duplicate would be retained postpolyploidization. Overall, our study provides new insights into the processes of plant duplicate loss, retention, and functional divergence and highlights the need for further understanding factors controlling duplicate gene fate.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Consequences of Whole-Genome Triplication as Revealed by Comparative Genomic Analyses of the Wild Radish Raphanus raphanistrum and Three Other Brassicaceae Species" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/26/5.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Polyploidization events are frequent among flowering plants, and the duplicate genes produced via such events contribute significantly to plant evolution. We sequenced the genome of wild radish ( Raphanus raphanistrum ), a Brassicaceae species that experienced a whole-genome triplication event prior to diverging from Brassica rapa . Despite substantial gene gains in these two species compared with Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata , ∼70% of the orthologous groups experienced gene losses in R. raphanistrum and B. rapa , with most of the losses occurring prior to their divergence. The retained duplicates show substantial divergence in sequence and expression. Based on comparison of A. thaliana and R. raphanistrum ortholog floral expression levels, retained radish duplicates diverged primarily via maintenance of ancestral expression level in one copy and reduction of expression level in others. In addition, retained duplicates differed significantly from genes that reverted to singleton state in function, sequence composition, expression patterns, network connectivity, and rates of evolution. Using these properties, we established a statistical learning model for predicting whether a duplicate would be retained postpolyploidization. Overall, our study provides new insights into the processes of plant duplicate loss, retention, and functional divergence and highlights the need for further understanding factors controlling duplicate gene fate.

*   ### Glossary
    
    mya
    :   million years ago
    WGD
    :   whole-genome duplication
    WGT
    :   whole-genome triplication
    OG
    :   orthologous group
    SVM
    :   Support Vector Machine
    AUC
    :   Area Under Curve
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information" />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Polyploidization events are frequent among flowering plants, and the duplicate genes produced via such events contribute significantly to plant evolution. We sequenced the genome of wild radish ( Raphanus raphanistrum ), a Brassicaceae species that experienced a whole-genome triplication event prior to diverging from Brassica rapa .   <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2014 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">Polyploidization events are frequent among flowering plants, and the duplicate genes produced via such events contribute significantly to plant evolution. We sequenced the genome of wild radish (<em>Raphanus raphanistrum</em>), a Brassicaceae species that experienced a whole-genome triplication event prior to diverging from <em>Brassica rapa</em>. Despite substantial gene gains in these two species compared with <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> and <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, ∼70% of the orthologous groups experienced gene losses in <em>R. raphanistrum</em> and <em>B. rapa</em>, with most of the losses occurring prior to their divergence. The retained duplicates show substantial divergence in sequence and expression. Based on comparison of <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>R. raphanistrum</em> ortholog floral expression levels, retained radish duplicates diverged primarily via maintenance of ancestral expression level in one copy and reduction of expression level in others. In addition, retained duplicates differed significantly from genes that reverted to singleton state in function, sequence composition, expression patterns, network connectivity, and rates of evolution. Using these properties, we established a statistical learning model for predicting whether a duplicate would be retained postpolyploidization. Overall, our study provides new insights into the processes of plant duplicate loss, retention, and functional divergence and highlights the need for further understanding factors controlling duplicate gene fate.</p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-13">All angiosperms are polyploids or have experienced a polyploidization event in their recent evolutionary history (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Ramsey and Schemske, 1998</a>; <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Jiao et al., 2011</a>), resulting in multiplication of their gene content. These duplicated genes may remain functionally redundant briefly but will eventually be retained due to new function gains (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Ohno, 1970</a>) or due to partitioning of ancestral functions (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Force et al., 1999</a>) or will be lost through deletion or other processes leading to pseudogenization (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Li et al., 1981</a>). Aside from these mechanisms, the retention of duplicate genes may also be attributed to the selection for balanced gene drive/gene balance (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Freeling and Thomas, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Birchler and Veitia, 2007</a>), functional buffering (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Chapman et al., 2006</a>), dosage selection (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Conant and Wolfe, 2008</a>), and/or escape from adaptive conflict (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Des Marais and Rausher, 2008</a>; reviewed in <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Edger and Pires, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Innan and Kondrashov, 2010</a>). The retention of duplicates, especially those derived from polyploidization, is correlated with certain gene functions (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Blanc and Wolfe, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Hanada et al., 2008</a>), gene complexity (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Chapman et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Jiang et al., 2013</a>), levels of gene expression (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Pál et al., 2001</a>), parental genome dominance (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Chang et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Schnable et al., 2011</a>), and network connectivity (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Thomas et al., 2006</a>). Despite correlations of these features with duplicate retention, it remains unclear to what extent these features may explain duplicate retention. This issue can be addressed in greater detail in Brassicaceae due to the close evolutionary relationship between species in the Brassiceae tribe, including wild radish (<em>Raphanus raphanistrum</em>) and <em>Brassica rapa</em>, and those in the <em>Arabidopsis</em> genus (diverged 43 million years ago [<a id="xref-def-1-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a>]; <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>). Also, a broad range of molecular data in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> can be used to infer the potential roles of Brassiceae duplicates. In addition, there is a recent hexaploidization event in the Brassiceae lineage (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Lagercrantz and Lydiate, 1996</a>), allowing a closer look at the patterns of duplicate loss and retention.</p><p id="p-14">In Brassicaceae, studies of duplicate genes in <em>A. thaliana</em> suggest three rounds of whole-genome duplication (<a id="xref-def-2-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a>) occurred after its lineage diverged from the monocot lineage. The most recent <a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event (α) occurred 50 to 65 <a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Bowers et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>), prior to the divergence of species in the Brassicaceae family. Notably, a further hexaploidization event (hereafter referred to as the α’ whole-genome triplication [<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>] event) occurred recently in the common ancestor of <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-38-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Lagercrantz and Lydiate, 1996</a>; <a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Lysak et al., 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Yang et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Town et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>). Among Brassiceae species, much of the knowledge about the evolution of α’ duplicates is derived from species in the <em>Brassica</em> genus (<a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>). Since the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>, &gt;50% of the <em>Brassica</em> duplicates may have been lost via deletion and pseudogenization, some of which has occurred in a biased fashion (<a id="xref-ref-70-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Tang et al., 2012</a>). These findings provide a baseline understanding of duplicate evolution post <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> and raise additional questions regarding rate of pseudogenization of duplicate genes and patterns of expression divergence.</p><p id="p-15"><em>R. raphanistrum</em> is native to the Mediterranean region and is a close relative of the cultivated radish (<em>Raphanus sativus</em>). The wild radish has evolved a weedy form that is a global agricultural pest (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Warwick and Francis, 2005</a>), and it is also a model for the study of ecology and evolution (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Sahli et al., 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Conner et al., 2009</a>). Thus, availability of genomic and transcriptomic resources for <em>Raphanus</em> will contribute to a better understanding of the molecular basis and evolutionary characteristics of weediness and aid in improvement of cultivated radish. In addition, these resources enable comparative genomic and transcriptomic analyses between <em>Raphanus</em>, <em>Brassica</em>, and <em>Arabidopsis</em> species to understand evolution of duplicate genes post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>. In this study, we report the draft assembly and annotation of the <em>Raphanus</em> genome and ask four major questions. First, what are the patterns of gene loss and retention post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em>? Second, how may pseudogenes in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genomes provide information on gene death post triplication? Third, what is the extent of duplicate gene expression divergence? Finally, can we computationally predict which genes would be retained or lost after duplication? Our results suggest that the patterns of evolution of α’ duplicates are similar in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> and that the retention process possesses biases that can be exploited for computationally predicting whether a duplicate would be lost or retained post polyploidization.</p></div><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Sequencing and Assembly of the Wild Radish Genome</h3><p id="p-16">As the first step in generating a draft assembly for the <em>R. raphanistrum</em> (referred to as <em>Raphanus</em>) genome, we estimated the genome size of <em>Raphanus</em> using flow cytometry. The estimated size of 515 Mb is comparable to genome size estimates of related species, including <em>Brassica</em> (529 Mb), <em>Brassica oleraceae</em> (696 Mb), and <em>R. sativus</em> (573 Mb) (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Johnston et al., 2005</a>). We sequenced the genome of a 5th generation inbred plant, and the reads were assembled with a hybrid approach (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 1</a>). The final assembly size was 254 Mb, representing 49.3% of the estimated genome size, with an N50 of 10.1 kb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). This is comparable to the draft <em>B. rapa</em> (referred to as <em>Brassica</em>) genome where the assembly is 283.8 Mb, or 53.7% of the estimated genome size, despite its significantly better sequencing coverage of 72× (<a id="xref-ref-70-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>). The size of the euchromatic space in <em>Brassica</em> is estimated to be ∼220 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Mun et al., 2009</a>). In addition, ∼30% of all <em>Brassica</em> chromosomes are composed of centromeric repeats that occupy ∼50% of all heterochromatic domains (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Lim et al., 2007</a>). Assuming that most of this heterochromatin consists of repetitive, nongenic regions and <em>Raphanus</em> is similar to <em>Brassica</em> in its heterochromatin content, it is likely that we captured most of the nonrepetitive genome space in our <em>Raphanus</em> assembly.</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/49878/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/49878/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <span class="caption-title">Comparison between <em>Raphanus</em> and <em>Brassica</em> Assemblies</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-20">Using the MAKER annotation pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Cantarel et al., 2008</a>), we predicted 38,174 protein coding genes in the <em>Raphanus</em> assembly (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 2A and 2B</a>). The coverage of the gene space in our <em>Raphanus</em> and the published <em>Brassica</em> assembly was further assessed using ESTs and using the core eukaryotic gene mapping approach (<a id="xref-ref-53-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Parra et al., 2007</a>). We found that 96.9 and 94.2% of the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> unique ESTs could be mapped to their respective assemblies (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). In addition, the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> assemblies contained complete matches for 248 (100%) and 241 (97.2%) core eukaryotic genes, respectively (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). These observations suggest that the <em>Raphanus</em> assembly is less complete and more fragmented than <em>Brassica</em>. However, a significant proportion of the gene space in <em>Raphanus</em> is covered in the draft assembly. For further comparisons of the gene space across species, we employed a combination of similarity and synteny-based approaches to define orthologous groups (<a id="xref-def-4-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>) between <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, <em>Brassica</em>, and <em>Raphanus</em> protein-coding genes (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 3</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Timing the Speciation and Polyploidization Events in Brassicaceae</h3><p id="p-21">To understand the patterns of duplicate gene evolution before and after the genome triplication event, it is important to know the <em>Brassica</em>-<em>Raphanus</em> speciation time in relation to the timing of the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> and <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> speciation events. Previous studies have suggested a broad range of timings for speciation and the <a id="xref-def-3-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> events in the Brassicaceae family (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 4A</a>), and some of these estimates have been revised based on availability of new data (<a id="xref-ref-4-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>). Due to inconsistent times and methodological differences between these studies, we used the most recent published data to reestimate the timing of the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event and the timing of the <em>Brassica</em>-<em>Raphanus</em> speciation event. Using two dating methods, the first based on synonymous substitution rate (<em>d<sub>S</sub></em>) and the second based on Bayesian approximation, the median divergence times between <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> were estimated to be 13 and 19 <a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a>, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1A</a>). These estimates are older than the predicted divergence of <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> (10 and 11 <a id="xref-def-1-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a>) and more recent than the divergence time between the <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> lineages (32 and 36 <a id="xref-def-1-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1B</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Synonymous Substitution Rate (dS) and Relationships between Brassicaceae Species.(A) dS between ortholog pairs and between paralogs derived from &#x3B1;&#x2019; WGT among Brassicaceae species.(B) Timing of polyploidization (blue circle) and speciation (open circles) events. The first and second numbers corresponding to each event are estimated based on the dS and Bayesian dating approaches, respectively. Thickness of the solid lines corresponds to the genome size. (The image for A. lyrata is used with permission, &#xA9;Ya-Long Guo, Max Planck Institute for Developmental Biology.)" class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1001220240" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Synonymous Substitution Rate (&lt;em&gt;d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/em&gt;) and Relationships between Brassicaceae Species.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/em&gt; between ortholog pairs and between paralogs derived from &#x3B1;&#x2019; WGT among Brassicaceae species.&lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Timing of polyploidization (blue circle) and speciation (open circles) events. The first and second numbers corresponding to each event are estimated based on the &lt;em&gt;d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/em&gt; and Bayesian dating approaches, respectively. Thickness of the solid lines corresponds to the genome size. (&lt;em&gt;The image for A. lyrata is used with permission, &#xA9;Ya-Long Guo, Max Planck Institute for Developmental Biology.&lt;/em&gt;)&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Synonymous Substitution Rate (<em>d<sub>S</sub></em>) and Relationships between Brassicaceae Species.</p><p id="p-23"><strong>(A)</strong> <em>d<sub>S</sub></em> between ortholog pairs and between paralogs derived from α’ <a id="xref-def-3-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> among Brassicaceae species.</p><p id="p-24"><strong>(B)</strong> Timing of polyploidization (blue circle) and speciation (open circles) events. The first and second numbers corresponding to each event are estimated based on the <em>d<sub>S</sub></em> and Bayesian dating approaches, respectively. Thickness of the solid lines corresponds to the genome size. (<em>The image for A. lyrata is used with permission, ©Ya-Long Guo, Max Planck Institute for Developmental Biology.</em>)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">These estimates are significantly older than some of the previous estimates (13 to 24 <a id="xref-def-1-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> for <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> split) for two reasons. First, the Bayesian prior and the lower limit for the divergence time between <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>Brassica</em>/<em>Raphanus</em> lineages we used is based on the most recent fossil data (<a id="xref-ref-4-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>). The second reason is that we used a lower but more recent neutral substitution rate estimate (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Ossowski et al., 2010</a>) than some of the earlier studies (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 4B</a>). Using α’ <a id="xref-def-3-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>-derived <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> duplicates, we estimated that the <a id="xref-def-3-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event took place 24 and 29 <a id="xref-def-1-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1B</a>). Our estimates are in agreement with some previous studies, which estimated the <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> split to have occurred 33 to 43 <a id="xref-def-1-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> and the <a id="xref-def-3-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event 22 to 29 <a id="xref-def-1-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> (<a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Town et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-4-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Couvreur et al., 2010</a>). Taken together, our results suggest that the polyploidization event likely occurred 3 to 12 million years after the separation of the <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> lineages and that the <em>Raphanus</em> genus may have been diverging from <em>Brassica</em> for a longer time than previously estimated (<a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Yang et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-43-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Lysak et al., 2005</a>). In addition, the α’ duplicates may have 5 to 16 <a id="xref-def-1-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> of shared ancestry, followed by 13 to 19 <a id="xref-def-1-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> of independent evolution in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em>.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Patterns of Loss and Retention of Duplicate Genes Post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a></h3><p id="p-26"><em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> have 27,416 and 32,670 annotated protein-coding genes, respectively. Assuming that the common ancestor of <em>A. thaliana</em>/<em>A. lyrata</em>/<em>Brassica</em>/<em>Raphanus</em> had ∼30,000 genes, the α’ event should have created ∼90,000 genes. Considering that there are 41,174 <em>Brassica</em> and 38,174 <em>Raphanus</em> annotated genes, only ∼42 to 45% genes from the ancestral hexaploid have been retained. The extent of gene loss is evident at the protein domain level because there are, on average, 1.4 times more domain family members in both <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> versus <em>Arabidopsis</em> species instead of the expected three times more (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2A</a>). Next, we examined the patterns of duplicate gene retention at the level of <a id="xref-def-4-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>, where each <a id="xref-def-4-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> specifies one ancestral gene that existed prior to the divergence of the Brassiceae species examined. We identified 16,567 <a id="xref-def-4-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> containing high-confidence <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genes derived from the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 3</a>). Based on these <a id="xref-def-4-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> definitions, both the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> lineages have experienced gene losses in ∼70% of the <a id="xref-def-4-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>, returning them to a singleton or complete gene loss state (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2B</a>). Among 10,521 and 8871 <a id="xref-def-4-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> returned to a singleton state in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em>, respectively, 6235 (70.3%) <a id="xref-def-4-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> overlap, which is significantly higher than random expectation (Fisher’s exact test P &lt; 1e-16).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Patterns of &#x3B1;' Duplicate Evolution.(A) Comparison of PFAM domain family sizes between species pairs. Each dot corresponds to the number of genes possessing a particular PFAM domain. The numbers in red and blue indicate the slope of the best fit line (red line) and the R2 value, respectively.(B) Comparison of OG sizes between the four species. Each row indicates the number of genes from each of the four species (column) in an OG.(C) Schematic representations of Type I and Type II OGs. AT, Arabidopsis thaliana; AL, Arabidopsis lyrata; BR, Brassica rapa; RR, Raphanus raphanistrum." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1001220240" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Patterns of &#x3B1;' Duplicate Evolution.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Comparison of PFAM domain family sizes between species pairs. Each dot corresponds to the number of genes possessing a particular PFAM domain. The numbers in red and blue indicate the slope of the best fit line (red line) and the &lt;em&gt;R&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sup&gt;2&lt;/sup&gt; value, respectively.&lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Comparison of OG sizes between the four species. Each row indicates the number of genes from each of the four species (column) in an OG.&lt;strong&gt;(C)&lt;/strong&gt; Schematic representations of Type I and Type II OGs. AT, &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/em&gt;; AL, &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis lyrata&lt;/em&gt;; BR, &lt;em&gt;Brassica rapa&lt;/em&gt;; RR, &lt;em&gt;Raphanus raphanistrum.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F2.medium.gif" width="183" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 2." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F2.medium.gif" width="183" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/49862" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 2.</span> <p id="p-27" class="first-child">Patterns of α' Duplicate Evolution.</p><p id="p-28"><strong>(A)</strong> Comparison of PFAM domain family sizes between species pairs. Each dot corresponds to the number of genes possessing a particular PFAM domain. The numbers in red and blue indicate the slope of the best fit line (red line) and the <em>R</em><sup>2</sup> value, respectively.</p><p id="p-29"><strong>(B)</strong> Comparison of <a id="xref-def-4-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> sizes between the four species. Each row indicates the number of genes from each of the four species (column) in an <a id="xref-def-4-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a>.</p><p id="p-30"><strong>(C)</strong> Schematic representations of Type I and Type II <a id="xref-def-4-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>. AT, <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>; AL, <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>; BR, <em>Brassica rapa</em>; RR, <em>Raphanus raphanistrum.</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-31">The presence of such common singletons may be due to common gene losses in the ancestral branch leading up to the <em>Brassica</em>-<em>Raphanus</em> last common ancestor or independent, parallel losses in these two lineages post speciation. To distinguish between these two possibilities, information from the <em>Brassica</em> homoeologous blocks (<a id="xref-ref-70-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>) was jointly analyzed with <em>Brassica-Raphanus</em> ortholog assignments. In the previous study, 27,774 <em>Brassica</em> genes were assigned to 22,546 orthologous and homoeologous relationships (<a id="xref-ref-70-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>), of which 21,170 (76.6%) genes in 19,036 relationships are common to our stringently defined set of homologs. Each homoeologous relationship represents one ancestral gene prior to genome triplication; thus, at least 35,938 (19,036 times 3 minus 21,170) and up to 49,000 (30,000 times 3 minus 41,000) genes were lost from the <em>Brassica</em> genome since <a id="xref-def-3-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>. Of the 21,170 retained <em>Brassica</em> genes, 2912 (13.7%) genes do not have <em>Raphanus</em> orthologs. The likely explanation is that independent losses of these <em>Raphanus</em> orthologs took place post <em>Brassica</em>-<em>Raphanus</em> speciation. Based on this relaxed definition of independent loss, we estimate that as many as 86% of the losses may have occurred in the shared lineage of both species. Because the criterion for calling independent losses is relaxed, this estimate of losses in the shared lineage may be considered an upper limit.</p><p id="p-32">To address the question of whether <a id="xref-def-4-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> with retained duplicates have distinct properties from those with singleton genes, we classified the <a id="xref-def-4-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> into three types (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2C</a>). The type I <a id="xref-def-4-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (2534) contain only one member from <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> and one member from <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em>, excluding <a id="xref-def-4-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> containing tandem or segmental duplicates. Type II <a id="xref-def-4-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> contain one member each from <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> and two or three members from <em>Brassica</em> or <em>Raphanus</em>. Type III consists of the remaining <a id="xref-def-4-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (9331). <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genes in type I and type II <a id="xref-def-4-18" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> are referred to as singletons and retained duplicates, respectively. We found that more of the retained duplicates tend be involved in biotic and abiotic stress response, hormonal signaling, development, as well as regulation of transcription, compared with singletons (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 5A</a>). In contrast, singletons were enriched in processes such as DNA repair, cell division, metabolic processes, as well as RNA modification and processing (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 5B</a>). These results are consistent with previous findings in <em>Brassica</em> and other flowering plants (<a id="xref-ref-70-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">De Smet et al., 2013</a>).</p><p id="p-33">Our findings indicate that a large percentage of <a id="xref-def-4-19" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (∼70%) experienced losses in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em>, returning them to a singleton state with significant functional bias. Such a behavior may be expected as the polyploid became diploidized over the past 27 million years. To better understand the process of gene loss, we identified and analyzed pseudogenes in the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genomes.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Pseudogenization of Duplicate Genes</h3><p id="p-34">Studies on synthetic and naturally occurring recent polyploids suggest that newly formed polyploids may undergo rapid genomic arrangements and chromosomal losses in the first few generations (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Shaked et al., 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Tian et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Matsushita et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Chester et al., 2012</a>), which can result in instantaneous loss of several thousand genes from the genome via deletion. Another mode of gene loss is through accumulation of substitutions and/or small indels in the gene body leading to pseudogenization. We identified 39,659 <em>Brassica</em> and 21,226 <em>Raphanus</em> pseudogenes that are fragments of their paralogs (&gt;80% covered only &lt;50% of the paralog length) and/or contain premature stops/frameshifts (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3A</a>; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 6 and 7A</a>). To assess the error rate in misclassifying a gene as a pseudogene, four analyses were conducted (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 6</a>). The predicted pseudogenes have significantly higher <em>d<sub>N</sub></em>/<em>d<sub>S</sub></em> values compared with functional ortholog and paralog pairs (Kolmogorov–Smirnov test P &lt; 1e-15; <a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3B</a>). Although some pseudogenes had <em>d<sub>N</sub></em>/<em>d<sub>S</sub></em> values comparable to functional duplicate genes, these pseudogenes contain in-frame stops and/or frameshifts or are short fragments (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 7A</a>), suggesting that they are not simply false positives but may have been created recently.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Patterns of Pseudogenization in Brassicaceae Species.(A) Number of pseudogenes (&#x3A8;) predicted in each species, before (red) and after (blue) correcting for the fragmented nature of the genomic assemblies.(B) Evolutionary rates (dN/dS) of orthologs between A. thaliana (AT), A. lyrata (AL), Brassica (BR), and Raphanus (RR) and between paralogs in BR and in RR. The paralog rates were calculated between pairs of annotated, presumably functional paralogs and between functional gene-pseudogene pairs.(C) Timing of pseudogenization (black and gray lines) compared with the timing of other events." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1001220240" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Patterns of Pseudogenization in Brassicaceae Species.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Number of pseudogenes (&#x3A8;) predicted in each species, before (red) and after (blue) correcting for the fragmented nature of the genomic assemblies.&lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Evolutionary rates (&lt;em&gt;d&lt;sub&gt;N&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;d&lt;sub&gt;S&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;/em&gt;) of orthologs between &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; (AT), &lt;em&gt;A. lyrata&lt;/em&gt; (AL), &lt;em&gt;Brassica&lt;/em&gt; (BR), and &lt;em&gt;Raphanus&lt;/em&gt; (RR) and between paralogs in BR and in RR. The paralog rates were calculated between pairs of annotated, presumably functional paralogs and between functional gene-pseudogene pairs.&lt;strong&gt;(C)&lt;/strong&gt; Timing of pseudogenization (black and gray lines) compared with the timing of other events.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F3.medium.gif" width="216" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F3.medium.gif" width="216" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/49893" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-35" class="first-child">Patterns of Pseudogenization in Brassicaceae Species.</p><p id="p-36"><strong>(A)</strong> Number of pseudogenes (Ψ) predicted in each species, before (red) and after (blue) correcting for the fragmented nature of the genomic assemblies.</p><p id="p-37"><strong>(B)</strong> Evolutionary rates (<em>d<sub>N</sub></em>/<em>d<sub>S</sub></em>) of orthologs between <em>A. thaliana</em> (AT), <em>A. lyrata</em> (AL), <em>Brassica</em> (BR), and <em>Raphanus</em> (RR) and between paralogs in BR and in RR. The paralog rates were calculated between pairs of annotated, presumably functional paralogs and between functional gene-pseudogene pairs.</p><p id="p-38"><strong>(C)</strong> Timing of pseudogenization (black and gray lines) compared with the timing of other events.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-39">To determine whether pseudogenization is still ongoing among α’ duplicates, we estimated the timing of pseudogenization for the pseudogenes derived from the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event. First, we stringently defined 2268 <em>Brassica</em> and 1261 <em>Raphanus</em> pseudogenes as derived from α’ <a id="xref-def-3-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> because they are located in homoeologous regions with their annotated, presumably functional paralogs. Given that we see ∼40,000 genes in each genome, ∼50,000 genes may have been lost from the neopolyploid ancestor, which may have had ∼90,000 genes (assuming the common ancestors of the four Brassiceae species had 30,000 genes). Thus, our prediction of 1000 to 2000 α’ <a id="xref-def-3-18" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> derived pseudogenes is an underestimate. To estimate timing of pseudogenization, a method was used assuming that the two duplicate genes experience the same degree of selective constraint before pseudogenization, and the pseudogenized copy evolves neutrally (see Methods; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 7B</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Chou et al., 2002</a>). The number of pseudogenized duplicates is higher after α’ <a id="xref-def-3-19" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>, but we do not see a sharp increase in pseudogenization immediately after the <a id="xref-def-3-20" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event. Instead, we find a gradual pattern of pseudogenization wherein some pseudogenes were formed very recently (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3C</a>). The choice of criteria for defining α’ derived pseudogenes did not significantly affect this pattern (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 7D to 7G</a>). These results suggest that pseudogenization is ongoing even 27 million years after <a id="xref-def-3-21" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>.</p><p id="p-40">Our results also suggest that there was no peak of pseudogenization soon after α’ <a id="xref-def-3-22" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>. However, this analysis has two caveats. First, because we can detect only ∼2000 α’ <a id="xref-def-3-23" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> pseudogenes, we may have missed older pseudogenes that have degraded beyond recognition. Second, related to the first issue, our analysis is limited to gene loss via pseudogenization and that gene loss via whole-gene deletion may have a different profile, contributing differently to overall gene loss compared with pseudogenization. Hence, the relative rates of loss via deletion versus pseudogenization need to be further studied.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Sequence Divergence of Duplicate Genes Post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-24" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a></h3><p id="p-41">Although a large proportion of the triplicated gene content has been lost, ∼15% of the duplicates are still retained. Over the past 27 million years, these retained duplicates may have subfunctionalized or neofunctionalized via sequence or expression divergence. To detect sequence level divergence, we performed a relative rates test on the protein sequences using an amino acid substitution model and found that 13.1 and 18.7% of the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> gene pairs, respectively, experienced asymmetric evolution (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 8A</a>). The asymmetrically evolving duplicate gene pairs were also found to have a 1.5× higher <em>d<sub>N</sub></em>/<em>d<sub>S</sub></em> ratio than duplicate pairs evolving at a uniform rate (Kolmogorov–Smirnov test P &lt; 1e-15) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 8B</a>), which is similar to observations in yeast (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Fares et al., 2006</a>), cotton (<em>Gossypium hirsutum</em>), and <em>A. thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Cronn et al., 1999</a>; <a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Blanc and Wolfe, 2004</a>).</p><p id="p-42">These results suggest that functional divergence in almost a fifth of the duplicate gene pairs may have occurred via asymmetric sequence divergence. It is likely that this is an underestimate because the power to detect asymmetry is reduced for older duplicates, shorter sequences, and asymmetry with small effect sizes (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Seoighe and Scheffler, 2005</a>). Also, instances of asymmetry on both branches at different times, which effectively negate each other, or phenomena such as gene conversion, will be hidden from our analysis. Nevertheless, our results suggest a substantial degree of sequence divergence that may significantly impact gene function. It has been suggested that there is a burst of protein sequence evolution immediately after a <a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event, and the genes that evolve fast initially tend to experience a more relaxed selective regime than the slow evolving ones, for a long time after the <a id="xref-def-2-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Scannell and Wolfe, 2008</a>). Such an accelerated rate of protein evolution, which leads to a rapid accumulation of independent mutations in the duplicate gene copies may set the stage for asymmetric sequence evolution of duplicates over time.</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Expression Divergence of Duplicate Genes Post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-25" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a></h3><p id="p-43">Duplicate genes may diverge not only in sequence but also expression (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Conant and Wolfe, 2008</a>). To understand the extent of expression divergence in α’ duplicates, we used gene expression data from <em>A. thaliana</em> flowers and <em>Raphanus</em> floral buds and asked (1) if <em>Raphanus</em> genes show signatures of expression level divergence when compared with their <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs and (2) whether the expression patterns are different between 1:1, 1:2, and 1:3 <em>A. thaliana</em>:<em>Raphanus</em> <a id="xref-def-4-20" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>. Based on the expression distribution of <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genes, we partitioned their expression levels into five states, very low (0 to 20%), low (20 to 40%), medium (40 to 60%), high (60 to 80%), and very high (80 to 100%), as well as a sixth “not expressed” state, and examined transitions between states for pairwise <em>A. thaliana</em>:<em>Raphanus</em> comparisons. Defining <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> orthologs with the same expression state as conserved, 36.6% <em>Raphanus</em> genes in 1:1 <a id="xref-def-4-21" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> have a conserved expression state, which is significantly higher than randomly expected (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4A</a>; z-scores range from 10 to 35 among blocks along the diagonal). On the other hand, the degree of expression state conservation drops substantially among <em>Raphanus</em> retained duplicates in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-22" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> compared with 1:1 <a id="xref-def-4-23" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>. Assuming that the <em>A. thaliana</em> ortholog expression level represents the ancestral expression level, there are apparently significantly more transitions from a higher expression state to a lower one among <em>Raphanus</em> retained duplicates in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-24" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4A</a>).</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Expression Divergence of &#x3B1;' Duplicates.(A) Z-scores of % overlaps between A. thaliana (AT) and Raphanus (RR) expression states compared with fitted distributions of % randomly expected overlaps (10,000 trials). NE, not expressed; VL, very low; LO, low; MD, medium; HI, high; VH, very high. Red, overrepresentation; blue, underrepresentation.(B) Observed and expected distributions of reads per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (RPKM) ratios between RR and AT orthologs in the three OG types. The horizontal dotted line indicates the baseline according to the observed ratio in the 1:1 OG type. The branchwise observed values (blue) were calculated first by sorting orthologs in an OG based on their expression levels. Orthologs with lower expression levels also have smaller branch number designations. The expected values (red) were obtained by randomly shuffling the association between AT and RR orthologs for each OG type. The observed totals over all branches (white) were calculated using the sum of the RR ortholog RPKM values in an OG." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1001220240" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Expression Divergence of &#x3B1;' Duplicates.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Z-scores of % overlaps between &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; (AT) and &lt;em&gt;Raphanus&lt;/em&gt; (RR) expression states compared with fitted distributions of % randomly expected overlaps (10,000 trials). NE, not expressed; VL, very low; LO, low; MD, medium; HI, high; VH, very high. Red, overrepresentation; blue, underrepresentation.&lt;strong&gt;(B)&lt;/strong&gt; Observed and expected distributions of reads per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (RPKM) ratios between RR and AT orthologs in the three OG types. The horizontal dotted line indicates the baseline according to the observed ratio in the 1:1 OG type. The branchwise observed values (blue) were calculated first by sorting orthologs in an OG based on their expression levels. Orthologs with lower expression levels also have smaller branch number designations. The expected values (red) were obtained by randomly shuffling the association between AT and RR orthologs for each OG type. The observed totals over all branches (white) were calculated using the sum of the RR ortholog RPKM values in an OG.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F4.medium.gif" width="202" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F4.medium.gif" width="202" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/50096" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4.</span> <p id="p-44" class="first-child">Expression Divergence of α' Duplicates.</p><p id="p-45"><strong>(A)</strong> Z-scores of % overlaps between <em>A. thaliana</em> (AT) and <em>Raphanus</em> (RR) expression states compared with fitted distributions of % randomly expected overlaps (10,000 trials). NE, not expressed; VL, very low; LO, low; MD, medium; HI, high; VH, very high. Red, overrepresentation; blue, underrepresentation.</p><p id="p-46"><strong>(B)</strong> Observed and expected distributions of reads per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (RPKM) ratios between RR and AT orthologs in the three <a id="xref-def-4-25" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> types. The horizontal dotted line indicates the baseline according to the observed ratio in the 1:1 <a id="xref-def-4-26" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> type. The branchwise observed values (blue) were calculated first by sorting orthologs in an <a id="xref-def-4-27" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> based on their expression levels. Orthologs with lower expression levels also have smaller branch number designations. The expected values (red) were obtained by randomly shuffling the association between AT and RR orthologs for each <a id="xref-def-4-28" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> type. The observed totals over all branches (white) were calculated using the sum of the RR ortholog RPKM values in an <a id="xref-def-4-29" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-47">Our findings indicate that <em>Raphanus</em> genes in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-30" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> have experienced higher degrees of expression level divergence since the <a id="xref-def-3-26" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event, while those in 1:1 <a id="xref-def-4-31" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> tend to have conserved expression levels in flowers. In addition, most instances of expression divergence between <em>Raphanus</em> and <em>A. thaliana</em> genes in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-32" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> are in the form of state transitions to lower expression levels in one or more of the <em>Raphanus</em> branches (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4A</a>). Assuming expression level increases and reductions are equally likely among <em>Raphanus</em> paralogs in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-33" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (red box plots, <a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4B</a>), significantly more cases of expression level reduction are observed than randomly expected (Kolmogorov–Smirnov test P &lt; 1e-15 in all comparisons) (blue box plots, <a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4B</a>). More importantly, despite expression divergence, the <em>Raphanus</em> copies with the highest expression levels within <a id="xref-def-4-34" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (branch 2 in 1:2 and branch 3 in 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-35" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a>) appear to maintain the ancestral expression level. This inference is made because the ratio between the highest expressing <em>Raphanus</em> duplicate to its <em>A. thaliana</em> ortholog in both 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-36" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (blue boxes, <a id="xref-fig-4-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4B</a>) is similar to the median ratio in 1:1 <a id="xref-def-4-37" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> (horizontal dotted line, <a id="xref-fig-4-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4B</a>). Thus, after genome triplication, one duplicate likely maintains the ancestral level of expression while the other retained copies have reduced expression in a particular tissue. Such expression differentiation may be reflective of functional differentiation occurring in the retained duplicates through sub- or neofunctionalization.</p><p id="p-48">We also found that the sum of expression levels among the retained <em>Raphanus</em> duplicates in 1:2 and 1:3 <a id="xref-def-4-38" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> was higher than the expression level of their <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs (unfilled box plots, <a id="xref-fig-4-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4B</a>). Assuming the expression of the <em>A. thaliana</em> ortholog is similar to the ancestral level, these results suggest that the total expression level of all duplicates in an <a id="xref-def-4-39" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OG</a> may not be subjected to strong selection to match the expression level in the ancestral gene. This finding, however, does not rule out the possibility that dosage balance is important because the balance may occur at posttranscriptional and posttranslational levels. For example, retained duplicates may possess different efficacies of performing the same function (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Nowak et al., 1997</a>), and dosage balance can be established at the level of protein activity. We also note that our results are obtained from analyzing only floral tissues in two species from two different studies and that the assumed preduplication ancestral expression level may not be the same as the expression level of the <em>A. thaliana</em> ortholog. Although comparing between 1:1, 1:2, and 1:3 orthologs may reduce the influence of cross-species/cross-study biases, a more stringent definition of ancestral expression state based on data for all four species under the same conditions in multiple developmentally similar tissues will provide a more complete picture of duplicate expression evolution.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Informative Features Correlated with α and α’ Duplicate Retention</h3><p id="p-49">Our results so far indicate that duplicates in ∼15% of the <a id="xref-def-4-40" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">OGs</a> may have been retained post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-27" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a>. Such retained duplicates may exhibit functional (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 5A and 5B</a>) or other biases (<a id="xref-ref-52-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Pál et al., 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Chapman et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Schnable et al., 2011</a>). One unanswered question is whether some of these features are better predictors of duplicate retention than others. To address these questions, we first examined five types of gene features, including GO-Slim classification, sequence-related features, expression-related features, network-related features, and conservation-related features (see Methods; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a>). For each feature, we asked if the feature values of retained duplicates were significantly different from those of singletons. In addition, we compared the properties of α’ retained duplicates and singletons against those derived from the α <a id="xref-def-2-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event (<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Bowers et al., 2003</a>). Because the general trends in <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> are essentially the same, in all subsequent discussions we discuss the joint results of both species.</p><p id="p-50">With some exceptions, most features are consistently over- or underrepresented among retained duplicates between the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-28" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> and α <a id="xref-def-2-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> events (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>). For example, among biological functions, retained duplicates are most strongly enriched in GO-Slim categories related to transcriptional regulation, stress response, signal transduction, and transport for both polyploidization events (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>). Compared with singleton genes, duplicates retained after the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-29" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event tend to have larger gene sizes (P &lt; 1e-9), higher GC3 content (P &lt; 1e-21), higher expression levels (P &lt; 1e-25), and broader expression profiles (P &lt; 1e-3). They also tend to be responsive to biotic and abiotic stresses (P &lt; 1e-7 and P &lt; 1e-4, respectively) and have greater network connectivity (P &lt; 1e-21). In addition, compared with singletons, retained duplicates tend to have homologs in a higher number of land plant genomes (P &lt; 1e-45) and lower <em>d<sub>N</sub></em>/<em>d<sub>S</sub></em> values compared with their <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs (P &lt; 1e-24). The observation that genes with greater network connectivity and with signal transduction and regulatory functions are retained may indicate a tendency to maintain dosage balance among certain genes, as per the predictions of the gene balance hypothesis (<a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Freeling and Thomas, 2006</a>). Biased retention of genes possessing these properties may lead to conservation and subsequent functional divergence of duplicated gene modules through time, in turn resulting in increased morphological and physiological diversity in polyploid lineages (<a id="xref-ref-26-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Freeling and Thomas, 2006</a>).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparison of Features between Retained Duplicates and Singletons.(A) Features with overrepresented (red) or underrepresented (blue) numbers of retained duplicates according to multiple testing corrected Fisher&#x2019;s exact test P values (Q-values). The value distributions of some features were divided into four quartiles (shades of gray). Names of certain GO-Slim categories marked with an asterisk have been abbreviated as noted in Supplemental Methods.(B) The AUC-ROC (Receiver Operating Characteristic) for the &#x3B1; WGD (blue) and &#x3B1;' WGT (red) duplicate retention prediction models using all features in (A).(C) Comparison of the SVM weight of the &#x3B1; WGD and the &#x3B1;' WGT models. Informative features (|weight| &gt; 0.05) in a consistent direction between the &#x3B1; and the &#x3B1;' models are colored blue while those in opposite direction are colored red. Numbers correspond to feature IDs noted in (A)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1001220240" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparison of Features between Retained Duplicates and Singletons.&lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt; Features with overrepresented (red) or underrepresented (blue) numbers of retained duplicates according to multiple testing corrected Fisher&#x2019;s exact test P values (Q-values).
