Informative features (|weight| &gt; 0.05) in a consistent direction between the &#x3B1; and the &#x3B1;' models are colored blue while those in opposite direction are colored red. Numbers correspond to feature IDs noted in &lt;strong&gt;(A)&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F5.medium.gif" width="178" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F5.medium.gif" width="178" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/26/5/1925/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/50068" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-51" class="first-child">Comparison of Features between Retained Duplicates and Singletons.</p><p id="p-52"><strong>(A)</strong> Features with overrepresented (red) or underrepresented (blue) numbers of retained duplicates according to multiple testing corrected Fisher’s exact test P values (Q-values). The value distributions of some features were divided into four quartiles (shades of gray). Names of certain GO-Slim categories marked with an asterisk have been abbreviated as noted in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a>.</p><p id="p-53"><strong>(B)</strong> The <a id="xref-def-6-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a>-ROC (Receiver Operating Characteristic) for the α <a id="xref-def-2-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> (blue) and α' <a id="xref-def-3-30" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> (red) duplicate retention prediction models using all features in <strong>(A)</strong>.</p><p id="p-54"><strong>(C)</strong> Comparison of the <a id="xref-def-5-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">SVM</a> weight of the α <a id="xref-def-2-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> and the α' <a id="xref-def-3-31" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> models. Informative features (|weight| &gt; 0.05) in a consistent direction between the α and the α' models are colored blue while those in opposite direction are colored red. Numbers correspond to feature IDs noted in <strong>(A)</strong>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Predicting Duplicate Gene Retention</h3><p id="p-55">The enrichment analyses indicate that some features are significantly different between retained duplicates and singletons, many of which are consistent between α and α’ events. Two questions remain. The first is regarding the relative importance of these features in differentiating retained duplicates from singletons. Second, our results are consistent with a recent study investigating gene retention across multiple plant <a id="xref-def-2-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> events (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Jiang et al., 2013</a>), raising the question whether a predictive, unifying model for the process of gene retention can be generated computationally by combining multiple gene properties. To address these questions, we considered all features (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a>) and generated predictive models for the α <a id="xref-def-2-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> and the α’ <a id="xref-def-3-32" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> events using a machine learning algorithm, Support Vector Machine (<a id="xref-def-5-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">SVM</a>; see Methods). The model performance was evaluated using Area Under Curve (<a id="xref-def-6-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a>) where a perfect model will have an <a id="xref-def-6-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> of 1 and a random model will have an <a id="xref-def-6-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> of 0.5.</p><p id="p-56">For the model predicting α’ duplicate retention using all features (the full model), the average <a id="xref-def-6-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> is 0.73, which is significantly better than the model constructed with randomized data (average <a id="xref-def-6-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> = 0.51; <a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5B</a>) or using single sets of features (the individual models, average <a id="xref-def-6-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> = 0.56; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9B</a>). The results are similar for α duplicates, although, compared with random guesses, the performance in classifying α duplicates (average <a id="xref-def-6-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> = 0.75) is slightly better than predicting α’ duplicates (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9A</a>), likely because GO-Slim, expression features, and network features for the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genes were inferred from their <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs. We also found that excluding one feature set at a time from the full model (“leave-one-out” models) did not significantly affect the model performance <strong>(</strong>average <a id="xref-def-6-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> = 0.72; <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9B</a>). Thus, combining multiple features into a single model allows for a better classification of retained duplicates from singletons than models based on random guesses or single features. Next, we asked whether models generated based on training data of the α’ event can be used to predict retention of α duplicates and vice versa. The model trained on the α’ data set generated an average <a id="xref-def-6-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> of 0.61 when used to classify α duplicates, while the model trained on the α data set generated an average <a id="xref-def-6-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> of 0.67 for α’ duplicates. While both AUCs are better than the individual models and random guesses, the performance of these models is significantly worse than the models trained and used to predict retained duplicates from the same event, suggesting the presence of unique properties of retained duplicates associated with each <a id="xref-def-2-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event. This is consistent with the results of enrichment tests, which showed variable degrees of over- and underrepresentation for different feature types (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5A</a>). In addition, some features have positive <a id="xref-def-5-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">SVM</a> weights (associated with better prediction of duplicates) for the α’ duplicates but negative weights (associated with better predictions of singletons) for the α duplicates (<a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5C</a>), indicating divergent properties between α and α’ events.</p><p id="p-57">Overall, these observations suggest that a three-feature set, including sequence-related features, Gene Ontology, and conservation-related features, allows us to generate a reasonable model for predicting gene retention. We find that models constructed using additional features do not perform better (average <a id="xref-def-6-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> α = 0.75, compared with average <a id="xref-def-6-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">AUC</a> α = 0.73 in models using the three-feature set). Thus, additional features, such as interaction partners and expression profile, depending on the <a id="xref-def-2-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event under study, may or may not lead to further improvement. In addition, there is an issue of overfitting as the number of parameters in the model increases. Because the three-feature sets can be readily obtained in sequenced species (perhaps with the exception of Gene Ontology categories that are inferred mostly based on conservation), the machine learning approach can be broadly applied to model gene retention across various polyploidization events in a quantitative manner. More importantly, the model performance provides a measure of our current state of understanding regarding factors affecting duplicate retention. Based on our findings, there are additional factors beyond the three-feature set that might contribute to predicting duplicate gene retention, but these factors have yet to be modeled and/or discovered.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Summary of Findings, Implications, and Unanswered Questions</h3><p id="p-58">In this study, we sequenced the genome of <em>R. raphanistrum</em>, a wild relative of the cultivated crops <em>R. sativus</em> and <em>Brassica</em>. This genome sequence, together with other sequenced Brassicaceae species (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, 2000</a>; <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Dassanayake et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Hu et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-70-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Wang et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Cheng et al., 2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Haudry et al., 2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Slotte et al., 2013</a>), makes Brassicaceae a highly desirable plant family for comparative genomic analyses. The 254-Mb assembly of <em>Raphanus</em> encompasses ∼49% of the estimated genome size, has an N50 of 10.1 kb, and includes a majority (38,174) of the genes in the <em>Raphanus</em> genome. We found that ∼60% of the genes in the neopolyploid ancestor of <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> were lost since the <a id="xref-def-3-33" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> event; however, several thousand genes are still retained within the <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Raphanus</em> genomes and may contribute to evolutionary novelty. For example, a recent study showed that circadian rhythm regulated genes are preferentially retained in <em>Brassica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Lou et al., 2012</a>), suggesting the possibility of phenological changes in post α’ <a id="xref-def-3-34" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">WGT</a> species. In our study, retained duplicates were found to possess functions related to transcriptional regulation, stress regulation, and development. Retention of duplicates may lead to the immediate evolution of novel functions that can be adaptive and allow conquest of new ecological niches. Alternatively, the retention of these genes can be due to subfunctionalization (<a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Force et al., 1999</a>) and dosage balance (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Birchler and Veitia, 2007</a>), which may not involve the evolution of new functions in the short term but may pave a path toward eventual neofunctionalization (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">He and Zhang, 2005</a>).</p><p id="p-59">What are the properties of retained duplicates? Over the past decade, several studies have taken advantage of the increased availability of genome sequence data and comparative genomic tools to analyze the evolution of <a id="xref-def-2-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a>-derived duplicate genes in multiple species, assessing features important for the loss and retention (<a id="xref-ref-6-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Blanc and Wolfe, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-57-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Schnable et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Jiang et al., 2013</a>). In this study, we confirmed the features assessed as important in earlier studies and determined their relative importance in distinguishing duplicates from singletons using machine learning. Our framework identifies features consistently correlated with gene loss/retention across the α and α’ duplicates. We found that although existing knowledge is useful for building predictive models of duplicate retention, the model performance is far from perfect, suggesting additional features are important for explaining the gene retention process. Examples of missing features may include subgenome bias (<a id="xref-ref-57-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Schnable et al., 2011</a>), importance of dosage balance (<a id="xref-ref-26-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Freeling and Thomas, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Birchler and Veitia, 2007</a>), or simply random loss. In addition, a recent study suggests that retained duplicates from one <a id="xref-def-2-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event have only a 50% chance of being retained after a subsequent <a id="xref-def-2-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> event (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Schnable et al., 2012</a>). Modeling using such additional features may help provide a more complete picture of gene retention and loss post <a id="xref-def-2-16" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a>.</p><p id="p-60">One counterintuitive finding in our analyses is that retained α and α’ duplicates tend to have lower evolutionary rates compared with singletons. This phenomenon has been noted before in plants (<a id="xref-ref-10-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Chapman et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Sémon and Wolfe, 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Jiang et al., 2013</a>). One explanation is that retained duplicates may provide a buffering effect against perturbation of essential functions under certain circumstances (<a id="xref-ref-49-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Nowak et al., 1997</a>; <a id="xref-ref-10-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Chapman et al., 2006</a>), and selection for such buffering may constrain the rate of duplicate evolution (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a> for discussion). Another possibility is that, if duplicate genes were retained due to selection for maintaining proper dosage in macromolecular complexes, accumulation of nonsynonymous substitutions in any of the components in the complex may disturb the established stoichiometry, a phenomenon that might be selected against (<a id="xref-ref-26-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Freeling and Thomas, 2006</a>). Considering that retained duplicates tend to have higher network connectivity, broader and higher expression, and certain biological functions, these properties may lead to higher retention probability due to a need to maintain dosage among network, coexpression, and functional modules, respectively.</p><p id="p-61">Our results also suggest a complex pattern of expression evolution between retained duplicates in <em>Raphanus</em>, where one of the triplicates tends to have a similar expression state as its <em>A. thaliana</em> ortholog, while other copies have reduced expression level. We found that the sums of <em>Raphanus</em> duplicate or triplicate expression levels are in general higher than their <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs. This suggests that, at least at the transcriptional level, a “dosage imbalance” can persist for more than 20 million years after polyploidization. However, our study involves the transcriptome from only one organ, and expression divergence among retained duplicates needs to be investigated in more detail using transcriptomic data from more tissues/conditions. In recent years, genomic and transcriptomic data from multiple plant species, many of which have undergone recent or ancient polyploidization events, have been made available. Comparative analyses of pseudogenes and duplicate genes derived via <a id="xref-def-2-17" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGD</a> events and their expression patterns in these species will provide a comprehensive picture of the loss/retention/divergence process in plants.</p></div></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-12"><h2 class="">METHODS</h2><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Estimating Genome Size</h3><p id="p-62">The procedure used to analyze nuclear DNA content in plant cells was modified from a previously published study (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Arumuganathan and Earle, 1991</a>). For flow cytometry, 50 mg fresh leaf tissue was sliced into 0.25- to 1-mm segments in a solution containing 10 mM MgSO<sub>4</sub>.7H<sub>2</sub>O, 50 mM KCl, 5 mM HEPES, pH 8.0, 3 mM DTT, 0.1 mg/mL propidium iodide, 1.5 mg/mL DNase free RNase (Roche), and 0.25% Triton X-100. The suspended nuclei were filtered and incubated at 37°C for 30 min. The sample nuclei was spiked with standard nuclei and analyzed with a FACScalibur flow cytometer (Becton-Dickinson). We used multiple DNA standards including Chicken Red blood cells (2.5 pg/2C), <em>Glycine max</em> (2.45 pg/2C), <em>Oryza sativa</em> cv Nipponbare (0.96 pg/2C), and <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (0.36 pg/2C). For each sample, the propidium iodide fluorescence area signals (FL2-A) from 1000 nuclei were collected. The mean position of the G0/G1 (Nuclei) peak and the internal standard were determined by CellQuest (Becton-Dickinson).</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing, Assembly, and Annotation</h3><p id="p-63"><em>Raphanus</em> is an obligate outcrosser. To reduce the amount of heterozygosity in the genome, <em>R. raphanistrum</em> subspecies <em>raphanistrum</em> (weedy) from the Binghampton population in New York was inbred for five generations and sequenced using Illumina Genome Analyzer II. Sequence reads were preprocessed and assembled with a combination of ABySS 1.2.5 (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Simpson et al., 2009</a>), Newbler 2.5.3 (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Margulies et al., 2005</a>), the Celera Assembler 6.1 (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Miller et al., 2008</a>), and Minimus2 from AMOS 3.1.0 package (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Sommer et al., 2007</a>) (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 1</a>). The MAKER 2.10 pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Cantarel et al., 2008</a>) was used to annotate the <em>Raphanus</em> assembly as detailed in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 2</a>A. Functional annotations of gene models were obtained using BLAST2GO (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Conesa et al., 2005</a>). The assembled genome and annotations are available at <a href="http://radish.plantbiology.msu.edu">http://radish.plantbiology.msu.edu</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>EST Sequencing and Assembly</h3><p id="p-64">ESTs were sequenced from three <em>R. sativus</em> cultivars (convars <em>sativus</em>, <em>caudatus</em>, <em>oleifera</em>) and four <em>R. raphanistrum</em> populations (subspecies <em>raphanistrum</em> NY weedy, <em>raphanistrum</em> Central Spain, <em>maritimus</em> Coastal Spain, and <em>landra</em> France populations). Total RNA from whole seedlings of <em>R. raphanistrum</em> and <em>R. sativus</em>, buds, and anthers was pooled together. Double-strand cDNA was synthesized from pooled RNA using SMART technology (Clontech). The prepared cDNA was normalized by cDNA denaturation/reassociation, treatment by duplex-specific nuclease, and amplification of the normalized fraction by PCR. The normalized cDNA was then digested with <em>Sfi</em>I, fractioned, directionally ligated into pDNR-LIB (Clontech), and electroporated into GC10-competent cells (Gene Choice). Sequences were generated from the 5′ and 3′ ends of clones. A total of 185.4 Mb and 310,844 ESTs were generated and deposited in National Center for Biotechnology Information (<a id="xref-def-7-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">NCBI</a>) dbEST.</p><p id="p-65">For assembly, 163,862 <em>Raphanus</em> and 213,105 <em>Brassica</em> EST sequences were downloaded from <a id="xref-def-7-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">NCBI</a> dbEST and were assembled into 106,152 and 85,508 unique transcripts, respectively, using a modified version of the PlantGDB pipeline (<a href="http://www.plantgdb.org/prj/ESTCluster/PUT_procedure.php">http://www.plantgdb.org/prj/ESTCluster/PUT_procedure.php</a>). Specifically, the ESTs were processed using a combination of Vmatch 2.1.7, TrimEST (EMBOSS package 6.4.0) and RepeatMasker 3.3.0 (coverage = 225 and divergence = 30). Unique transcripts ≤100 bp were removed and were mapped to their respective genomes using GMAP v2011-09-14 at 30% coverage and 90% identity thresholds. Among overlapping matches, only the longest ones were used for further analyses.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Orthology Inference</h3><p id="p-66">We determined orthologous groups between the four Brassicaceae species using a combination of two approaches: similarity based and synteny based. In the similarity-based approach, an all-against-all BLAST (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Altschul et al., 1997</a>) search was performed between protein sequences from eight species, and similar genes were assigned orthologous groups using multiple alignment followed by phylogenetic reconstruction. In the synteny-based approach, syntenic groups were first determined between the four species, and orthologous relationships were then defined among the syntenic groups using a phylogenetic approach (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 3</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Pseudogene Identification</h3><p id="p-67">A modified version of a previously defined pseudogene pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">Zou et al., 2009</a>) was used to predict pseudogenes in genomes of all four species under study (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 6</a>). The procedure first involves using protein sequences to search the genome. The matches are regarded as “pseudo-exons,” concatenated together and classified as pseudogenes. Using four different approaches, we confirmed that a significant majority of our pseudogene predictions were not false positives, i.e., real genes misclassified as pseudogenes. To account for false positive predictions as a result of the fragmentary nature of the <em>Raphanus</em> and <em>Brassica</em> genomes, we also eliminated pseudogene predictions lying close to contig ends.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Timing of Speciation, Duplication, and Pseudogenization</h3><p id="p-68">Two approaches were used to determine the speciation and duplication time. First, using a lower limit of <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> divergence time of 30 <a id="xref-def-1-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> (<a id="xref-ref-4-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>) as well as a neutral substitution rate of 7*10<sup>−3</sup> substitutions/site/million years (<a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Ossowski et al., 2010</a>), we performed Bayesian dating with a prior of 36 <a id="xref-def-1-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> for the <em>A. thaliana</em>–<em>Brassica</em> divergence time (<a id="xref-ref-68-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Town et al., 2006</a>). In the second approach, we obtained divergence times based on <em>d<sub>S</sub></em> and the neutral rate estimate indicated above (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 4A and 4B</a>). To estimate the timing of pseudogenization, we used a published approach (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 7B</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Chou et al., 2002</a>). All estimates ≤0 <a id="xref-def-1-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">mya</a> were discarded. To determine whether the timing was robust to the definition of α’ pseudogenes, we used four additional means of calling pseudogenes as α’ derived (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figures 7D to 7G</a>). We found no significant deviation from our proposed inferences.</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>RNA-seq Analyses</h3><p id="p-69">RNA-seq data from <em>Arabidopsis</em> flower (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Jiao and Meyerowitz, 2010</a>) was used. For <em>Raphanus</em>, 100 mg of buds of different sizes were used for RNA extraction using Qiagen RNEasy plant RNA mini kit and subsequent sequencing using Illumina Genome Analyzer using the standard library preparation protocol. The obtained 36-bp reads were quality filtered and mapped to the <em>Raphanus</em> genome as previously described using TopHat (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Trapnell et al., 2012</a>). Read counts per gene model were obtained using HT-Seq (<a href="http://www-huber.embl.de/users/anders/HTSeq/">http://www-huber.embl.de/users/anders/HTSeq/</a>), and the reads per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads value was obtained using custom scripts.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Classifying Retained Duplicates and Singletons with Machine Learning</h3><p id="p-70">We used an implementation of <a id="xref-def-5-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">SVM</a> (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Joachims, 1999</a>) to generate classifiers that allow distinguishing between retained duplicates and singletons. The feature sets used in this study are detailed in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a>. For expression-related features, we obtained data from previously published microarray (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Kilian et al., 2007</a>) and RNA-seq (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Filichkin et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Jiao and Meyerowitz, 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Moghe et al., 2013</a>) expression data sets in <em>A. thaliana</em>. For network-related features, we analyzed data from AraNet, a probabilistic functional gene network (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Lee et al., 2010</a>). If the feature values could not be obtained from <em>Brassica</em>/<em>Raphanus</em> directly, they were inferred from the <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs of the <em>Brassica</em>/<em>Raphanus</em> genes. See <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Figure 9</a> and <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1">Supplemental Methods</a> for more details.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Accession Numbers</h3><p id="p-71">Sequence data from this article can be found in the <a id="xref-def-7-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">NCBI</a> Sequence Read Archive under Bioproject PRJNA209513.</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>Supplemental Data</h3><p id="p-72">The following materials are available in the online version of this article.</p><ul class="list-simple " id="list-1"><li id="list-item-1"><p id="p-73"><a id="list-item-3"><p id="p-75"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.114.124297/DC1"><strong>Supplemental Figure 3.</strong></a> Divergence Time Estimates.</p></li><li class="fn-label">[W]</span> Online version contains Web-only data.</p></li></ul></div><div class="section glossary"><h3>Glossary</h3><dl id="def-list-1"><dt id="def-item-1">mya<span class="def-item-dt-sep"></span></dt><dd>million years ago<span class="def-item-dd-sep"></span></dd><dt id="def-item-2">WGD<span class="def-item-dt-sep"></span></dt><dd>whole-genome duplication<span
